In contrast to Arthur Conan Doyle's stories, where Holmes is mature and seasoned, in the films he is played as brilliant but wild and erratic.  His relationship with Watson is now an egalitarian friendship: the more stable Watson is the anchor that keeps Holmes sane. B005LAIHXQ|4.0|Prometheus was a titan who stole fire from the Gods, gave it to the humans and prevented Zeus from obliterating the human race. In punishment God chained him to a rock and bade an eagle perpetually eat his liver.<br /><br />In Ridley Scott's new film we open with sweeping aerial shots over a steaming, blasted landscape that may be Icelandic and may be Martian. We alight on a waxen figure of titanic Grecian aspect, standing astride a great waterfall, who may be Prometheus himself, \\"chained\\" to his \\"rock\\". Overhead, a higher power is concluding a visit. It does not appear to have been a cordial one.<br /><br />I do not know this for sure, and Scott does not explain further. That mysteries are more potent unexploded is a significant theme of this motion picture.<br /><br />We are pitched forward 35 millennia, to 2093, and into the face of Dr Elizabeth Shaw (Noomi Rapace), an anthropologist out in the field, clawing at an aperture into a concealed cavern with cavalier disregard for that theme. Dr Shaw and her partner Dr Charlie Holloway (Logan Marshall-Green) have discovered cave paintings suggestive of the intervention of an advanced civilisation. This is, of course, the stuff of Erich von D&auml;niken, fashionable as long ago as the 1970s but long since discredited. Nonetheless, Shaw and Holloway propose a multi-billion dollar, ten year expedition to a remote star system to further investigate. Elderly philanthropist Weyland (a rubber-suited Guy Pierce) green-lights the proposal.<br /><br />It must have been one hell of a business pitch.<br /><br />We jump forward a further four years and 50 billion light years (the laws of physics being due an overhaul between now and 2090) to the spaceship Prometheus, which has arrived at that star system. Without ado the ship fetches up on a blasted, steaming landscape, adjacent to a quite [[ASIN:0425166805 von D&auml;nikenesque]] archaeological complex.<br /><br />House android David (an excellently Hal-like Michael Fassbinder) and ship commander Meredith Vickers (Charlize Theron) are busily waking up their scientists and nursing hidden agendas. It quickly becomes clear that the crew of scientists, shaken from their stupor, have their own agendas too. Thus do we conform to the deep space adventure playbook established by the likes of [[ASIN:B000HEBCZQ 2001: A Space Odyssey]] and, well, Alien. Yet note a dissonance here: just as the dialogue starts in earnest, Scott shifts our gaze from the reverend contemplation of the immortal to the petty melodramas of some new characters with whom we cannot, yet, empathise. Naturally there are figurative parallels but, all the same, this shift in perspective risks rendering the cosmic mundane.<br /><br />I mention this because but for the wonderful production design and those grand metaphysical thoughts there is really nothing really new about the first hour of Prometheus. It is to Alien what [[ASIN:B000HF4U04 2010: The Year We Make Contact]] was to 2001. In place of ineffable profundity there is talky exposition.<br /><br />Ridley Scott renders the cosmic mundane in another way too: We are treated again to HR Giger's stunning necro-organic architecture, rendered briefly in Alien and largely unexplained in the form of a ghastly skeleton with a blown out rib-cage. But this time Scott indulges in exposition: he begins to fill in the details of this race of &Uuml;bermensch, ascribing them motives and agendas. We even meet one. It was better not knowing.<br /><br />In Alien the titillating ambiguity of their remnants left the job of world construction to the viewer. In Prometheus Scott takes away the viewer's autonomy, [[ASIN:B00498VS1E Alien]] dictates a back story, and opens himself wide to plot illogicalities and non-sequiturs. Disappointingly the Engineers' motivations are base, predictable, and not worthy of the phallic grandeur of Giger's production design.<br /><br />By half time in this two hour picture the studied monkishness of its opening has entirely given way to melodramatic space opera, rendered in haphazardly-directed action set pieces. As convention dictates, the dysfunctional crew must be picked off, but this is accompanied by none of the grimness of Scott's original, because the characters are only caricatures of the real men and women we met aboard the Nostromo.<br /><br />There are, generally, too many of them, and they spend too much of the picture gabbing at each other in the meter of stock Hollywood dialogue, depriving the screenplay of the air it needs to work up a genuine chill or to properly frame its grand eternal cogitations.  There are about three false summits before the final denouement, which falls on an offbeat not quite accented enough to justify its build up.<br /><br />This makes me sound too negative: I enjoyed Prometheus a great deal, but hoped to enjoy it immensely more still. It is a beautiful picture to look at, and many of the action set pieces are well executed (Noomi Rapace has her own John Hurt moment, self-inflicted, and it is in equal parts horrifying and (deliberately) hilarious.<br /><br />Ridley Scott has famously railed against studio demands to tinker with the final edits of his pictures. Here, I suspect he had full autonomy. I left the cinema wondering what might have been had someone been more assiduous in that regard: this is a good movie which could have been made great had there been a director more prepared to cut.<br /><br />Olly Buxtony to melodramatic space opera, rendered in haphazardly-directed action set pieces. B00005JKN9|2.0|There were so many basic errors in the historical facts, clothing, weapons, gangs themselves, the army, etc, that I could not enjoy it, the way one can with Braveheart or other good movies where the director doesn't let facts get in the way of the truth.  Most of the gangs of NY were Irish Catholic and fought among themselves in support of candidates, but deaths were rare.  Whole blocks were NOT the scenes of pitched battles with axes and guns and every woman wasn't a prostitute.  Its wasn't Irish Catholic vs Protestant.  The scenes of the army and navy in 1863 were not even close.  Scorsesee completely ignored those facts.  One thing he did get right was the attitude of the rich and some of the machine politicians of NY, and the basic attitudes of the poor Irish vs the draft and anything they believed made the draft necessary (like freeing the slaves and further competition for jobs).  There was no great pitched battle between \\"Americans\\" and \\"Irish\\" interupted by cannon shells and murderous soliders as depicted in the film.  The draft riots were simply police and the army vs the rioters who went on a rampage against the draft, killing negroes, police and setting fire to anything they perceived as supporting it (including a negro orphanage).  Anyone watching that movie came away with the opinion that the whole area was shelled by navy ships and shot and stabbed by soldiers as hundreds died in an hour or two, with a hundred more killed by the rival gangs. In reality, most of the deaths were poor blacks caught and killed as scapegoats.  The death toll was probably around 120, 80% of them black.  Five Points wasn't even part of the riots - that was centered on the areas where the draft machinery and establishment lived.<br /><br />Its too bad Scorsese could not figure out a way to tell the fantastic story correctly, make us smarter and entertain.  Nice costumes though...a few were even accurate.<br /><br />but the saddest thing is he did not pay homage to the spirit of the immigrant population themselves, as if all were out of work thugs or prostitutes.  Nothing could have been further from the truth.f the immigrant population themselves, as if all were out of work thugs or prostitutes.  Nothing could have been further from the truth.

0062119044|2.0|The story is told in what, accurately or not, I think most people imagine is the internal dialog of an intellectual woman living in New York City. Relentlessly self-examining but barren of any real change, tiresomely laden with rich vocabulary and the pensive tone of a person who is obsessively watching their own emotions from a literary distance, the voice of the protagonist - the mother of a teenage boy who has slaughtered everyone he doesn't like at his school - is difficult to like and to listen to for the whole book.<br /><br />The story seems like a depressing and horrible cautionary tale for women who aren't sure they want children - her reason for having her child has nothing to do with love for her husband and is openly, transparently, the selfish result of a kind of pernicious boredom.  Again, as I imagine people in New York to be, her implicit feelings of superiority over most other people and contemptuous disdain for religion, become wearing. It was also tiring and discouraging to read how consistently her husband denies the child's hostility, malice and hatred, and gradually discards his wife in order to protect his illusions about his son. The fact that the style of writing seems realistic makes this dissolution of a marriage seem realistic, too, as if the slow, painful emotional separation from a once-loving husband is as much a predictable outcome of a not-really-wanted pregnancy as is a son who premeditates and successfully carries out a mass murder.<br /><br />The internal monologue that shapes itself through the medium of letters also sometimes delivers commentary on our culture - we are characterized as lifeless watchers who \\"need\\" mass murderers to commit acts that can be endlessly watched on the news, in books, and in sensational dramatized movies.  While the commentary is not without value, it's such a narrow and grim view that it almost has the effect of creating or hardening a cynical, bitter view of humanity in general. After reading the book, if readers are not conscious of adopting these ideas as they are reading, they may find this dehumanizing outlook difficult to shake off.<br /><br />The letters to her husband are odd in that they don't seem to be addressing a person who will or does respond, so the surprise ending didn't surprise me very much at all.<br /><br />Ultimately, I found the book to be depressing and an ugly glimpse into a mind that, while being full of words and concepts, is spiritually empty. Because the antagonist, her son, and the story are so ugly also, I can not recommend this book.

0525478817|5.0|The Fault In Our Stars by John Green is a cleverly written, astonishingly touching, yet witty novel. Green's story features the life of several characters and how it is affected by Cancer. When writing about such an emotive subject as Cancer in teenagers it would be easy to fill a book with stereotypes and clichÃ©s. However, Green's work is much more than a book about Cancer.<br /> Green's novel is from 16 year old Hazel Grace Lancaster's point of view, a girl who has terminal lung cancer. She has an oxygen tank attached to her nose because in Hazels' words \\"My lungs suck at being lungs\\". Her life changes dramatically when she attends a Cancer support group and encounters Augustus Waters. As these two grow closer, their witty and thought-provoking conversations serve to lighten the mood of a novel surrounding a rather serious topic. They ask the questions that relate to the reader: Will I be loved? Will I be remembered? Will I leave a mark?<br /> The author's writing style has the ability to handle the pace of the novel appropriately and create characters with a voice that will touch the reader. Furthermore, Green makes The Fault In Our Stars an enjoyable read as he bases Hazel's journey not on the acceptance of death but on the acceptance of life. How life is not always fair, but life just is. The Fault In Our Stars is not a depressing read and it is not an uplifting novel either. It is simply a truthful depiction of love, loss, and the life of a young girl who has cancer.<br /> The Fault In Our Stars is an insightful and moving novel that will leave the reader with a lump in the throat, but with a more optimistic understanding of life.

The original series was a saga, a soap opera, but they had years to get through this material - here, you've got only over two hours and it happens so fast...until it happens so slow.  This is a film of fits.  One moment seventeen stories are going and then you get a long scene of nothing but one guy thinking to himself, staring slightly left to the camera and not sharing his thoughts.  The ending on the bridge as the final (last, totally, this it it, it's all over, people; watch me make the bold choice of going down with ship, it's really over, wait for it) sacrifice is about to be made - it goes on for forver, I feared it would never stop.  By the time the YAMATO finally is destroyed (taking the enemy down with it), I felt like a long stay in a boring room had finally come to an end.<br /><br />And this is another point - count the sets.  The Yamato is a big ship with a lot of room to move about, but the film limits us to a mere handful of rooms and most of those rooms you'll see are the main bridge (which, again, point of fact to the original series - you do see a lot of the bridge); which lacks any of the depth and interest of the original series.  It's grey, dull and the Captain's chair doesn't even go up and down (I waited the whole film for that, didn't happen).<br /><br />The Yamato itself is a high point in the film.  All the space shots, battles and the CGI work does, for any fan, send a true thrill through you.  It's beautiful, but so little time is spent with the ship, too few space battles and even less wave motion gun.  If you were to edit just the external scenes of the Yamato together it wouldn't run five minutes and that's plain wrong - the Yamato stands at the center of the story, here it's just something to be sacrificed at the end and you end up not caring.  It's one of the best ships in science fiction, even STAR TREK knows that people come just as much for the ENTERPRISE as the crew - here, not so much.<br /><br />A point on the music - as much as I love the original theme, it's used too much here to both over score and underscore the tension and mood.  It's played loud and brave in one moment then low and slow in the next (at points it borders on a dirge); there seems to have been little effort made to expand the score and what additions they have never rise high enough for you to even know it's there.  Whoever thought to get Steven Tyler from AREOSMITH to pen and sing the closing theme - put them in front of the wave motion gun and fire!, and then hit the ashes with the wave motion gun again, just to be sure.<br /><br />Overall, SPACE BATTLESHIP YAMATO takes too few chances and takes away far too much from the original for it to make any kind of connection.  It's too long, too unfocused and at its very worst feels just like an overhyped, pointless Hollywood production about battleships - we got that, don't need another.nt of the wave motion gun and fire!, and then hit the ashes with the wave motion gun again, just to be sure.<br /><br />Overall, SPACE BATTLESHIP YAMATO takes too few chances and takes away far too much from the original for it to make any kind of connection. What?  The baby-faced guy from \\"The Notebook\\"?  The nut from \\"Lars and the Real Girl\\"?<br /><br />Yes--the same guy. This is a very strange film, not easily definable, and not at all what you think it's going to be.<br /><br />It's romantic.  It's very violent.  It has several twists in it that I found either shocking or disturbing, or both.  And it's a film that makes no bones about making it extremely difficult to pin characters down into easy stereotypes.<br /><br />Don't worry: I won't provide any spoilers.  I'll just say this: Gosling plays a near silent man named/described as, simply, Driver.  An absolute whiz behind the wheel, when he's not busy at his day job as a car mechanic or a Hollywood stunt driver he moonlights as a man who will drive questionable guys where they need to go, no questions asked.  Here's how he puts it...<br /><br />\\"There's a hundred-thousand streets in this city. You don't need to know the route. You give me a time and a place, I give you a five minute window. Anything happens in that five minutes and I'm yours. No matter what. Anything happens a minute either side of that and you're on your own.\\"<br /><br />And that's almost all he says throughout the film.  But he really doesn't need to...he speaks pages with those great eyes, whether he's admiring Irene, watching over her young son Benicio, or staring the hell out of some rotten guy who is trying to hurt them.<br /><br />In the manner of the great Steve McQueen, Gosling is able to express an awful lot with those baby blues, and his near silence really works to establish him as a man who speaks rarely but is by no means inexpressive.  It's hard not to latch on to his iconic smile when he sees Irene, and to understand his feeling of dismay when he finds out she has an ex-con husband who's coming home from prison in a couple of days.<br /><br />And that's when the trouble begins.  It's easy to see that Driver does best when he lives on his own terms, but now he feels he needs to watch out for Irene's loser of a husband and that's when things begin to get ugly.  I mean, real ugly.  And Driver finds himself embroiled in a war he didn't want, but to his credit he won't back away, either.  It's all in those eyes: you just know he won't let Irene or Benicio down.<br /><br />There's quite a pedigree in this film, too: Carey Mulligan is Irene, Albert Brooks is a crime boss, as is his hair trigger partner Ron Perlman, and Bryan Cranston is Gosling's boss at the garage,  There are several other young actors of note here as well, but much credit has to go to the movie's director, Nicolas Winding Refn, a Danish director who is no stranger to violent movies about the dark underbelly of life, most recently directing 2009's \\"Bronson,\\" starring Tom Hardy (soon to become Bane, Batman's formidable nemesis in \\"The Dark Knight Rises.\\")  Refn does a fine job letting the film speak for itself, letting its actors walk a fine line between the notions of good and evil, making it very hard to pick absolutes within this film.<br /><br />In fact, when this movie is over it is very hard to clearly define anyone within this movie, and that includes Driver.  \\"Drive\\" is willing to allow itself to intermingle traditional concepts of right and wrong, and very few people can be easily categorized here.  It makes for interesting watching, and will probably make the film resonate for you for quite a while.<br /><br />If you hadn't guessed, I'm most impressed with Gosling, whom I'd thought was a simple hot chickflick guy who filled in the blanks when a romantic movie needed a hot dude. 0307592731|4.0|As a chicken who wouldn't even walk in a county park by myself, I was impressed at young Cheryl Strayed's decision to hike the Pacific Coastal Trail (think: left coast version of Appalachian Trail) at all, much less all alone, backpacking for two-plus months. At times it felt like an adult version of one of my most favorite books of all time, \\"My Side of the Mountain,\\" where the little boy runs away from home and hollows out a tree and makes his (quite cozy) home there for the whole winter.<br /><br />I enjoyed Strayed's accounts of how she did things, how she packed the enormous, too-heavy backpack, how she cared for her bruised, blistered feet, how she counted her pennies (literally) and every few weeks walked several miles into a town to pick up a package and have a real meal, maybe cadge a shower, before hitting the trail again.<br /><br />I loved her story of her mother, her mother's hard life (and therefore the author's hard life as a child) and tragic, too-young death, and Cheryl's anger at her mother as she comes to terms on the trail with her harsh, harsh loss(es).<br /><br />As with any memoir, I found myself wondering how, years later, the author remembers such tiny details and can write them accurately into a memoir. She doesn't mention keeping a diary on the trail. She mentions reading books and then burning the pages she reads, to lighten her load. But not writing in a journal. Yet she recounts tiny details, rocks, leaves, mountains, expressions on people's faces, in chronological order, things that took place over a 60-day period, 20 years ago.<br /><br />And as with any memoir, or fiction for that matter, some parts dragged for me. There was a point where we had heard it all before...the long walk, the setting up the tent, the hike into town, the disappointment when yet again a package isn't at the post office as hoped. I began to skim quickly about two-thirds of the way through, impatient with the repetition of wilderness descriptions and kind of inane and unimportant interactions with men she met on the trail. I was looking for the real meat of the story, which for me was Cheryl's coming to terms with why she was on that trail, and her grief about her relationship with her mom.<br /><br />Recommendation: Yes, a very satisfying read, she's a talented writer with a great story.<br /><br />Side note: I hear Reese Witherspoon has optioned the book and plans to star. 0140143505|5.0|84 Charing Cross Road is filled with the love of life and the love of books and is timeless and charming with a tinge of heartbreak. Helene Hanff wanted nice copies of used English books when she began corresponding with a used bookshop in London and she got more books than she could ever have imagined, but she also got several life-long friendships, beginning with the manager of the bookshop on Charing Cross Road, Frank Doel. Hanff was a writer in television, novels, textbooks and articles and well known in her time, in addition to being politically active. Hanff's correspondent was a mild-manner manager in a bookshop, beloved by all who knew him, but easily overlooked with the usual English reserve.<br /><br />Their correspondence, along with several other employees of the shop and extended family of employees covers more than two decades and begins with post-war rationing still in place and ends with screaming fans of the Beatles and the rise of Carnaby street - quite a progression in the England of the twentiety century. These letters are full of life and humor and every human emotion. The book reads quickly, but that is all right because as soon as the last page is turned, eager readers will begin again looking for favorite passages. Highly recommended to all sorts of readers but especially those who love English books and England.

0465068367|4.0|The title pretty much covers it.  This harrowing book tells the story of the Japanses conquest of the Chinese city of Nanking in late 1937.  Not for the faint of heart, Chang's story includes numerous detailed tales of rape, torture, and murder.  The handful of pictures in the book alone will turn your stomach.  The Japanese army's treatment of enemy soldiers and non-combatants rivals anything I have read on the Holocaust and the Eastern Front during the 1940's.  One of the most fascinating aspects of the story is that of John Rabe, a committed Nazi who, along with other westerners, made superhuman efforts to mitigate the damage being done to the city and its residents.  Chang's research appears thorough and she writes well.  My only quibble with the book is Chang's attempt to balance a historical account with a position piece on the lack of Japanese accountability after the war.  Chang's commentary on the latter is so heated that one cannot help wondering if the historical narrative is strictly objective.  That said, Chang is writing popular history, not a dissertation, and she succeeds admirably.  While reading the book I kept thinking how difficult it must have been for Chang to go through the source material.  I was thus saddened, but not shocked, to learn that she commmitted suicide a few years after this book was published.

Sometimes the transfer to Blu-ray simply lacks due care to be worthwhile.<br /><br />However there are occasions where extra effort has apparently been made and where all the above provisos no longer apply. This is one such film. The improvement is quite remarkable with a tremendous improvement apparent throughout the process of restoration. In particular the DVD letterbox imaging has been replaced with full screen imaging and the soundtrack has far greater range. Both imaging and sound have greater definition than offered on the previous DVD.<br /><br />Provided that reasonable allowance is made for the age of the original film stock, it would be reasonable to expect most purchasers to be more than satisfied with this transfer to Blu-ray. In my opinion this improvement is a real 5 star achievement and the restoration team deserves success for their efforts.

0743297334|4.0|This is my second Hemingway book of the past few months and I am very much a fan.  His writing is clear and the story never drags.  The book struck me as a \\"Seinfeld novel,\\" that is, an interesting story about nothing.  At the novel's beginning, Hemingway offers Gertrude Stein's famous statement about the \\"lost generation\\" of post-World War I Europe.  The characters in this story are definitely lost, or at least alcoholics.  Nearly every scene involves the consumption of a lot of alcohol; I was surprised any of the characters could stand, let alone relate Hemingway's crisp prose.  The Seinfeld comment aside, the novel centers on an American journalist who lives in France and travels to Spain with a group of friends.  All of the friends are damaged goods in various ways, but mostly they seem bored and heartbroken (not in the romantic sense of heartbreak, but the spiritual sense).  In the aftermath of the Great War they simply do not know how to comprehend life.  Careers, religion, and even love seem meaningless.  The people just exist -- and go to bullfights.  Indeed, the book is famous for Hemingway's vivid description of bullfighting in Spain; while the writing of these scenes is superb, I found myself cringing at the cruelty of the sport, which I suspect was not Hemingway's intention.  He would think I am being a weanie.  I am not sure I would widely recommend this book because of its somber tone, but if you're feeling a little lost and need something to relate to, give it a try.

0061743526|5.0|It seems to me there is no better teacher in this life than fresh and honest simplicity. And where better to get a candid view of life - all its quirks, little joys, terrible meanness, and what is truly important - than through the eyes of an unworldly, but wise, child (Scout). But here lies the power in Harper Lee's mid-20th century story, To Kill a Mockingbird. In one sense it offers little more than some visits over a few years with Scout, her brother, Jem, and their father, Atticus - along with associated kinfolk, neighbors, and small town notables - but it gives to you the feeling that you haven't been alone in how and what you felt as a child, and what you knew and always will know was right and wrong; and the irreconcilable despair you felt and feel when you haven't much control over the bad things that happen.<br /><br />Scout's family is somewhat enigmatic; her mother has passed away, her father is a lawyer and elected state government representative, and she and her brother are both well read, intelligent, and sensitive. This lends some of the objectivity to the story as she witnesses and experiences ignorance and disdain for non-conformance. It also provides clarity and incisiveness in illuminating readers about the extent of discrimination - very pervasive and practiced during this time period - against the town's black people, and in particular, a black man accused of a crime who is being represented by Scout's father.<br /><br />Knocking around from her adventures in elementary school, summer playtimes in outdoors - where imagination was the roux of recipes for fun, excitement, and inevitably trouble - visits with neighbors, Q & A with her lawyer-father, her relationships with the townspeople, etc. - the perennially inquisitive Scout collects and analyzes information and tries to make sense of it all.<br /><br />Told through dialogue, observations, and reflections that embody the simple and mundane activities of day to day life in small-town Alabama during the depression, the story always seems lively and fresh with Scout's sense of humor, as she struggles to understand the world she lives in and the people who are part of it. The closeness, honesty, and love shared by her small family - always in their own way - warms you like a wood fire on a chilly night, and leaves you with a glow with which to remember it.

Effects and editing are cleverly (if rather flashily) used to emphasise the heightened awareness, creating an oddly hallucinogenic mood as he transitions from down and out to super perceptive charmer and wheeler dealer.  In a supporting role Abbie Cornish does fine, and Robert de Niro shows up and says his lines in a throwaway role which seems almost perfunctorily added on.  Presumably he does just enough to get on the front cover to help sales, but it helps neither his career, or the film.<br />It's pretty exciting and relatively action packed, but with not too much violence.  All in all, any looming ridiculousness is staved off by a pace which doesn't allow you to think too much.  A shame perhaps, as with braver hands this might have been more of an interesting commentary rather than a thriller.. but it still works.  The directors name is Neil Burger.. perhaps if you consider the movie as cinematic fast food, it's a success.  It never felt like it had been put together by a masterchef, but it satisfied me at the time, reliably entertained me, even if I didn't remember much about it the next day.

0062012339|5.0|This book has gone down somewhat into the folklore of the hard rock and metal worlds as something of a must read and it's easy to see why. The Dirt sees one of the most vacuous success stories of 80's hard rock excess laying their souls down in print, discussing their early years and the struggles they had in order to emerge, festooned with mascara, from the primordial ooze of the Sunset Strip of the early and mid 80's and then taking that story further by discussing the travails of their later career up until circa 2001 when it was originally copyrighted.<br /><br />Rather than give you some tasty and tasteless highlights of the stories that are set forth in this book I'll just point out a few things about it. Firstly the book seems to have been done in a collaborative spirit in that the various band members `own' chapters and this gives each a chance to speak their mind. As mentioned earlier these stories range from early years through to mid and later career thoughts and points. Interspersed with these band member chapters there are also chapters devoted to business partners, managers and the like and some of hte chapters are in the form of interviews done with various external figures. All of which makes the book feel very rich and authoritative.<br /><br />Secondly the way the book is set out allows it to feel like a breezy read. None of these guys is Shakespeare though they do like to talk and Neil Strauss really is to be congratulated for knocking this into shape so well given how much of the book must have been sourced from innumerable conversations by people whose memories are almost certainly.... hit and miss shall we say.<br /><br />In terms of production values the typeface is easy on the eye and the regular black and white photographs of not only the band but all the miscellany of tour programs, tickets etc do make the whole thing seem quite well thought out and informative. A picture speaks a thousand words as the clich&eacute; says.<br /><br />As we all know this book is not the final word in that the band continued to operate past the date of its release.  Whether this means we'll get an expanded edition at some point in the future is totally open to conjecture but whatever the case it's certain that few rock `warts `n' all' tales will equal this one for blunt honesty (and some dishonesty I'm guessing). An excellent gift for anyone you know into rock `n' roll excess, who enjoys loud and proud hard rock or the like and has the stomach for lots of debauchery and the sort of language usually reserved for naval folk.w this book is not the final word in that the band continued to operate past the date of its release. 0451232577|4.0|In a small Welsh town of Aberowen, a 13 year old boy, Billy Williams, enters the dark pits of the world of coal mining. His sister, Ethel, is a housekeeper for the aristocratic Fitzherberts and makes the mistake of falling in love with Earl Fitzherbert, who is already married to Princess Bea of Russia. The Earl's sister, Lady Maud Fitzherbert, crosses into forbidden territory when she falls in love with a German diplomat. Two orphaned Russian brothers, Grigori and Lev Peshkov, dream of leaving Russia to emigrate to America but fate and the war affect their plans. And finally, the over-privileged American diplomat, Gus Dewar, who serves as an aide to Woodrow Wilson, plays his part in drawing America into the war.<br />The reader is taken into a riveting story that weaves together the lives of the main characters with each other and with real historical characters. As the story unwinds, so does the war.  The time period is well researched and serves as an informative history lesson on coal mining, trade unions, women's rights, attitudes of the aristocracy, politics, revolution and of course, the events that led up to the First World War.<br />I really like Ken Follett's development of characters, and I noticed this in Pillars of the Earth also. He portrays their motivations in such a way that the reader develops an ambivalent understanding for even the most unsympathetic characters, such as Lev Peshkov or the Earl.  None of the characters is perfect, and none are completely bad either. They all have their strengths and flaws that make them undeniably human and thus, relatable.<br />This is another magnificent, epic novel of historical fiction that I thoroughly enjoyed. A hefty book at 1000 pages, it would be easier to download on your slim kindle or ipad. Book two of the Century  trilogy is called Winter of the World...but I have not read it as yet. Enjoy!

B004Q7CIFI|5.0|In the afterword of Stephen King's remarkable \\"11/22/63,\\" King credits the late Jack Finney's \\"Time and Again\\" as the greatest time-travel story.  With all due respect to King's humility, not since H.G. Well's classic \\"The Time Machine\\" has this popular but tired topic been handled with as much creativity - and purpose - as this in-depth examination of Lee Harvey Oswald and the JFK assassination.<br /><br />Lewiston, Maine diner owner Al Templeton has stumbled upon a time portal in the pantry of his aging stainless steel diner.  Unlike many stories involving the science fiction of time travel, in this one the traveler has no control: a journey through the back of Al's Diner always ends at the same point:  September 9, 1958.  Templeton reasons that there is a short list of events that have had major impact on world history - the Kennedy assassination one of them.  So investing the five plus years in the past between 1958 to that fateful day in Dallas in 1963 to determine if Oswald really was working alone, and if so, to stop him, would be well worth the sacrifice.  But Templeton finds that the past is obdurate - it resists being changed.  So before completing his quest, he is stricken with terminal cancer, and it becomes very clear to him that Al will die before being able to complete the mission of his accidental journey.  Enter Jake Epping, English teacher at the local high school and casual friend of Al's.  Armed with Al's crazy story and his detailed notes, he steps - literally - through the pantry and back into 1958, just as Al described, and assumes the role of history-changer.  What follows is a rich and unexpected mix of nostalgia, history, love, and mystery, all wrapped in King's unparalleled knack for detail, subtle foreshadow, and suspense.  The imagery is superb: \\"Cocked on the back of his head was a filthy fedora that looked straight out of a 1950's film noir, the kind where all the women have big bazonkas and all the men talk fast around the cigarettes stuck in the corners of their mouths.\\" And as in much of King's work, inanimate objects take on sinister qualities: in this case, it is the past which proves to be a worthy, daunting, and malevolent adversary.<br /><br />I've observed that readers of fiction fall into two general categories - those who love Stephen King and those who can't tolerate him.  As I see it, \\"11/22/63\\" bridges these two groups; a fascinating blend of rich history and clever fiction that, while lacking the horror and terror expected from King, provides twists and turns and enough mystique to make this unmistakably Stephen King.  And strangely, this is also a love story; a tale complicated by the paradox of time; a sensitive treatment that avoids being maudlin while delivering a knockout blow as powerful and poignant as you'll ever read from accomplished authors known more for romance than terror.<br /><br />In short, \\"11/22/63\\" may be Stephen King's most important work; the culmination of over four decades of writing - an ambitious but superbly executed epic that dares to cross genres and succeeds on all fronts. 1451627289|3.0|I found 11/22/63 very enjoyable to read, but difficult in equal measure to swallow.<br /><br />It wasn't because of the writing, as I like the way King weaves a story, and I've read most of his books over the years and have always enjoyed them, especially this one being such an  endearing love story.  It wasn't because of the mechanics of time travel, as the conundrums time travel creates presents endlessly layered plot shifts to happily mess with our time-limited environment.<br /><br />What I did have difficulty with was King's premise regarding the killing of John F. Kennedy. 70-80% of Americans believe that JFK was killed by a conspiracy. The House Sub-Committee on Assassinations in 1979 concluded as much. A simple observation of the Zapruder film shows that the Single Bullet Theory, the tortured assumption that one bullet caused the injuries to both JFK and Governor Connally, is easily disproved. A bullet traveling at ~2000 fps did not linger in the air for almost 2 seconds waiting for the governor to assume the correct position to be injured. It did not crush and mangle bones in the Govenor's chest and wrist and then emerge in pristine condition. The SB Theory is the linchpin of the Warren Commission.  When shown that more than one bullet caused the injuries to these two men, its findings disintegrate. Occam's razor demands the simplest explanation based on the facts-more than one bullet for those injuries, more than one shooter caused them. Even Governor Connally and his wife Nellie, sitting beside him in the limousine that sunny Dallas morning, always disagreed with the Single Bullet Theory (aka Magic Bullet Theory).<br /><br />It was therefore hard to read the aspects of King's book based on the conventional myth presented by the Warren Commission--that Oswald alone killed JFK. And it is too important an issue to let slide as the continued lie slowly leaves people numbed to the truth--our president was killed by our own. An American coup d'etat.<br /><br />It does not matter what sort of screw-up Oswald was if the laws of physics and common sense show that there was more to the killing of JFK than a lone gunman. Another book, [[ASIN:0982906404 The Memoirs of John F. Kennedy: A Novel]], covers similar ground as 11/22/63. Released a year before, it comes at the subject from a more nuanced approach than the \\"Oswald did it\\" crowd and shows what might have happened had JFK survived the assassination attempt and gone on to a second term. What might have occurred had his real killers and their motives been exposed. The book has won numerous awards. I am the author. I am not a wild-eyed conspiracist. My comments here can be taken as self-promotion, as it is true that I desire more people to read my book. But more importantly, a more vigorous reckoning with the facts is needed.<br /><br />We Americans want to believe in our government. We want to believe we act morally and follow our best angels. But there are times when we must look behind the curtain and see our dark side. Attributing the death of JFK to Lee Oswald as a lone and independent sociopath is a convenient means of avoiding the hard truths behind the assassination. 50% of the American people disbelieved the findings of the Warren Commission when it was released in 1964. That number has only grown as more information and independent investigations have chipped away at the mis-information presented by that supposed Blue Ribbon panel. Extending the myth via this otherwise intriguing book by Stephen King is unfortunate.

She becomes the observer of herself and her stories, watching herself with humor, insight and growing compassion.<br /><br />I imagine that there will be many many more hikers hitting the trails after reading Strayed's story, but even for those who don't test themselves in the wilderness, there is still much to be learned just by living each day on the edge, Strayed-style. 0143035711|5.0|The story of his tour in Vietnam, like many other authors, Robert Mason is telling us that he experienced pride, passion, failure, and hope, but not victory. Like thousands of other Vietnam veterans, he was deprived of victory.<br /><br />The Army doctrine of using helicopters to transport troops and materiel into and out of \\"hot LZ's\\" (when under fire) is a sad tale of incompetence, poor planning and bad doctrine on the part of the Pentagon and the Army. This book is a very good handbook that all generals and field commanders should read and understand what not to do in similar tactical situations.<br /><br />Back in 1964-65 when I was in college and the bodies of American soldiers were coming home all too regularly in those steel cases, and there were funerals all over the country - I didn't fully understand. After college and with my own subsequent tour in Vietnam as a C-130 pilot, I have a better understanding ... but only better.<br /><br />Having myself been to all the locations (not the LZ's): Pleiku (pussy mountain), Kontum, Tuy Hoa, Qui Nhon, Can Tho, Saigon and every other city and town in South Vietnam, Cambodia and Thailand, that only had even a rudimentary dirt runway, I know more now; but I'm still ignorant.<br /><br />The harrowing conditions under which the Army helicopter pilots lived, fought and died is told in painful detail by Warrant Officer Mason. His journey from recruitment, officer training, and flight training is as told over and over by thousands of young American men (including this writer) in their transition from civilian life to experienced combat crewman in Vietnam.<br /><br />The lessons the national leaders should learn from Chickenhawk is that during an offensive, ground should be taken and held. Troops should not be sent into \\"hot LZ's\\" and then be extracted 24 to 72 hours later, just to let the enemy reoccupy the same ground. The tactic of massive helicopter assaults landing under fire, the shooting up of the helicopters and crew, as well as the grunts, should be abandoned as a flawed doctrine. It's a worthless waste of life and equipment as Mr. Mason has so eloquently stated.<br /><br />Michael W. Rea, Captain USAF<br />Savannah, Georgia

0674057929|5.0|Oscar Wilde's \\"The Picture of Dorian Gray\\" has appeared in nearly a dozen forms, some reflecting publishers' objections, some adding new material by Wilde to compensate for cuts, and some simply bowdlerized in pirate editions. For the first time, Wilde's original manuscript submitted for publication has been printed as he wrote and intended it, before other people's prejudices and pressure caused him to alter the text. This is a beautifully produced large format book, beautiful cover with Caravaggio's \\"Narcissus\\" cropped and darkened, detailed introduction, bibliography and analysis of the complete publishing history of this unusually tortured author and book. All variants are included in the notes and annotations. Of particular interest is the fact that it is this text, which has not previously been published, that Sir Edward Carson, Counsel for the brutal and sociopathic Marquis of Queensbury whom Wilde absurdly sued for libel over an open note in a men's club in which Queensbury, father of Wilde's infamous lover Bosie Douglas, wrote \\"Oscar Wilde posing as a somdomite (sic).\\" But for Wilde's utterly mad legal action against Queensbury, he would not have faced the tremendous wealth of testimony and written evidence placed before the civil court by Carson, and would not have been prosecuted for any crimes, as he was after losing his libel action. Many of the passages which appear only in this new edition of \\"The Picture\\" were used by Carson to paint Wilde into a corner of criminality; and it is interesting to see just which sentences and paragraphs were accumulated to impeach Wilde on cross-examination. All in all, this is a wonderful book, almost too grand for its subject matter. But then Oscar, I am sure, would have expected nothing less.

B0083SBJXS|5.0|Unless you are the sort of cinematic snob who can only appreciate avant-garde short subjects in tiny projection rooms, then chances are pretty good that you'll have a rollicking good time watching this 2012 megawatt adventure epic. I certainly did, and I wouldn't consider myself a die-hard Marvel comic book fan. I enjoyed the derring-do and ironic humor that infused the comic book films preceding this one, but director/screenwriter Joss Whedon really whips together all the formula elements that made [[ASIN:B004JMSIRC Iron Man]] (1 & 2), [[ASIN:B001LM1456 The Incredible Hulk]] (the 2008 version), [[ASIN:B0034G4P8A Thor]], and [[ASIN:B005IZLPMY Captain America: The First Avenger]] purely entertaining into something that miraculously makes the larger-than-life dimensions of the superheroes more instantly relatable.  A top-notch cast certainly helps as does the armada of technicians who pull off the effects in all their 3-D, eye-popping glory, of course, but much of the credit really needs to go to Whedon for making this film as much fun as a comic book.<br /><br />Running a surprisingly fleet 143 minutes, the potentially convoluted plot allows Nick Fury, a character heretofore limited to tantalizing post-credits scenes in the previous movies, to take center stage. He's the surly one-eyed leader of S.H.I.E.L.D., the peacekeeping agency who must quickly recruit a team of superheroes to do battle with Loki, the deceitful half-brother of Thor, the Norse deity of thunder. Loki has brainwashed archer supreme Hawkeye and brilliant physicist Dr. Erik Selvig (already seen in \\"Thor\\") while stealing a cosmic cube called the Tesseract, a powerful energy source of seemingly limitless potential, from a remote research facility. Fury recruits Black Widow, a former Russian spy last seen in action in \\"Iron Man 2\\", to gather the troops including a highly reticent Bruce Banner a.k.a. the Hulk. Meanwhile, Iron Man, Captain America and Thor have to learn to get along before facing the enemy.<br /><br />Loki turns out to be the least of their problems as the Tesseract unleashes a powerful alien race known as the Chitauri, which leads to the inevitable battle royale on the devastated streets of Manhattan. While the state-of-the-art action scenes are everything you want them to be, it's really the film's unexpected sense of humor that consistently buoys the story. Realizing the escalating level of implausibility, Whedon smartly provides clever lines and scenes to everyone (and not just Robert Downey, Jr.)  For instance, after Thor proclaims Loki solemnly as his Asgardian brother, Black Widow points out that he killed eighty people in two days to which Thor points out Loki was adopted. Later, in the most crowd-pleasing scene, the Hulk grabs a character by his leg and whip-slams him into the floor like he's beating a dust rag. The defiance of logic and physics is par for the course here, and Whedon's kid-like attitude and deft hand make it all the more exhilarating.<br /><br />The physically fit cast work surprisingly well together as an ensemble. While Downey's scene-stealing as Iron Man is a given, Chris Evans' Captain America, Chris Hemsworth's Thor, Mark Ruffalo's digitally altered Hulk, Scarlett Johansson's Black Widow, and Jeremy Renner's Hawkeye all get their moments to shine. As Loki, Tom Hiddleston effectively conveys the inferiority complex that drives his villain with slithery cunning, while Samuel L. Jackson and Clark Gregg take full advantage of expanded roles as Fury and Agent Coulson respectively. The mistake most filmmakers make in showing an Armageddon situation is applying too much soul-baring gravitas to the proceedings. That's definitely not Whedon's take as he never lets us forget that we are watching a comic book come to life. It's gratifying to watch a popcorn movie that actually lives up to the hype in all its commercial glory.y.<br /><br />Loki turns out to be the least of their problems as the Tesseract unleashes a powerful alien race known as the Chitauri, which leads to the inevitable battle royale on the devastated streets of Manhattan. 030788743X|5.0|This needs a 6-star rating, 5 stars just doesn't cut it.<br /><br />I received this book free though a Goodreads give-away.<br /><br />This book isn't going to appeal to everyone. Ready Player One relates the story of how a small group of virtual reality (OASIS) addicts fought against nasty corporate drones to seek out the Easter Egg set by the originator of the OASIS game environment. This was to be done by finding 3 keys and their subsequent gates using prodigious amounts of pop-culture from the past---mostly the 1980's as experienced by a Midwestern USA youth.<br /><br />A simple enough plot. What makes it magical is Ernest Cline's deft balancing of 80s trivia with his obvious love of geeky folk everywhere. In this regard it reminded me of the movie Galaxy Quest wherein a bunch of geeky SF fans save the day by virtue of just being themselves. Here in Ready Player One large numbers of people escape their dismal reality by venturing into an entirely immersive virtual reality simulator--and the geekiest of them seek out the prize laid down by the game's originator, James Halliday. A prize worth billions and carrying with it, the potential to save the world. Or just flee it.<br /><br />This book is like Stephenson's Snow Crash without the philosophy. B003N5VOUO|5.0|This movie has two parts to it, the love story between Rizwan Khan (Shahrukh Khan) and Miranda (Kajol), and Rizwan's quest to speak to the president and declare, \\"My name is Khan, and I'm not a terrorist.\\" The Rizwan-Miranda love story is portrayed as Rizwan's flashback while traveling to Washington D.C. to meet the president. I believe this technique was quite effective and did not disrupt the flow of the story. Both parts are just as good and gives variety to the film.<br /><br />Rizwan grew up as a Muslim in India and was later diagnosed with the mental illness called the Asperger's syndrome. After being sponsored by his brother in America to move to the states, he meets Miranda, a Hindu who falls in love and marries Rizwan. Just when everything seems perfect, the 9/11 attacks took place and the post-9/11 prejudice tears the couple apart. Miranda sarcastically tells Rizwan to tell the president that he is not a terrorist before he is allowed to see her again. Rizwan takes it seriously and begins his quest.<br /><br />In addition to the great plot, the movie incorporated good humor and religious significance. One thing I love about this film is that it attacks many stereotypes against Muslims and shows that Islam is not about violence. B0042X9L6A|5.0|I recently purchased the SB-700 to replace a faltering SB-600 that I purchased in 2005 to work with a Nikon D50. I now shoot with the Nikon D7000, and I've found the SB-700 to be an excellent companion to that setup. I'd venture to say, this is the best all-around choice for most people shooting a D3000/D5000/D7000 series DSLR from Nikon.<br /><br />OUTPUT<br />While the extra features are great, the ultimate measure of a flash is its output. Carrying the SB-600 since 2005, I always found its output to be more than adequate for my needs (event photography, and other mostly indoor or medium-range outdoor uses). Although the SB-600 is slightly more powerful than the SB-700, I've found the difference to be negligible at best. Bottom line, while the reach of the SB-400 is too shallow for me, I've found the SB-600/SB-700 to be the perfect balance of power, size, and function.<br /><br />USER INTERFACE<br />Especially when shooting with a Nikon body, the SB-700 is setup to simply work with your camera out of the box. There's really nothing to configure unless you want to dig into the more advanced features of this unit. Should you choose to dig into those features, you'll find Nikon has done a great job laying out the controls in a logical manner. Output patterns, and flash modes are each individual switches. The scroll wheel makes accessing, and using other on-screen features much easier than the navigation on my old SB-600. Overall, I'm really impressed with the SB-700's interface; I consistently forgot how to access certain functions on the SB-600, an issue I've yet to encounter with the SB-700.<br /><br />WIRELESS OPERATION (CREATIVE LIGHTING SYSTEM)<br />To help add more depth to your photos, the SB-700 can be triggered remotely as part of the Nikon Creative Lighting System (CLS). This has to be one of my favorite parts of the SB-700 when paired with my D7000. The SB-700 can serve as a Master or Remote (Slave) unit. While the Nikon CLS certainly isn't as robust as using Pocket Wizards, it's one of my camera's built-in functions. The power switch has Off, On, Remote, and Master positions, so putting the SB-700 in Remote (slave) mode is very easy. Switching the built-in flash on my D7000 to Master mode, I am now able to fire the SB-700 wirelessly (without anything other than my camera and the SB-700). The ability to remotely trigger a flash was not something I could do with my D50/SB-600 setup, and has added a whole new dimension to my photography.<br /><br />OTHER FEATURES<br />Shortly after purchasing my SB-600 in 2005, I also found a need for a diffuser. While a diffusion dome was a relatively inexpensive purchase, it was a separate purchase nonetheless. In unboxing the SB-700, I was happy to discover Nikon chose to include a diffusion dome with the SB-700.<br /><br />In fact, not only does Nikon include a diffusion dome with the SB-700, they also include an incandescent and fluorescent filter. These color the flash to help with white balance. In addition to this, I've also found the incandescent filter helpful for \\"warming up\\" the lighting on subjects.<br /><br />Another nice addition (over the SB-600) was the addition to an integrated reflector card. I would frequently carry an index card a rubber band with my SB-600 as another way to soften the light coming from my flash. The integrated reflector card within the SB-700 makes it a lot easier to use or not use a reflector card between shots, and certainly makes for a more professional presentation.<br /><br />In summary, I've found the SB-700 to be an incredibly solid unit. I'd venture to say this is the best general purpose flash for Nikon DSLR's. Unless you specifically need the additional output power of the SB-900/SB-910 units provide, the SB-700 is a very good choice for anyone shooting a D3000/D5000/D7000 series DSLR.

B000002B2X|4.0|Alice In Chains had put themselves on both the alternative and heavy metal maps with a pulverizing set of songs courtesy of their debut LP `Facelift'. As a follow-up, the band came out of left field with a mostly acoustic EP called `Sap'. Intended to be rough demos for future records, the quartet reconsidered and put `Sap' out warts and all in early 1992. `Sap' got little if any national recognition until the song \\"Got Me Wrong\\" appeared on the `Clerks' movie soundtrack two years later and became an alt-rock radio favorite.  Four songs, (not counting the mostly instrumental nonsense hidden track) with each song possessing a certain sense of the bleak but also each seemed to be filled with a tremendous harmonic strength.  \\"Brother\\" is probably my favorite but all are noteworthy including the all-star Seattle jam, \\"Right Turn\\". Ann Wilson also makes some notable vocal contributions to both \\"Brother\\" and \\"Am I Inside\\", keeping the Seattle connection alive and not limiting it to just the grunge underground. `Sap' is more than a happy accident and it is a worthwhile purchase for anyone who wants to know what the fuss was all about, from a true indie rock standpoint.

1594488843|1.0|My own view on Drive is that Pink gets a little right - but not a lot. It is a brilliant read, a lovely view, one that I would prefer would be dominate. But he is an author who wants to sell books and that is often best done by being disruptive, taking the opposing view and being controversial. We need people like Pink in the world to encourage corrective behavior when (in this case) people apply one particular model exclusive of all other considerations and approaches.<br /><br />My own firm belief is that in order to optimise motivation you have to apply some of Pink's view as a ingredient, like an onion in a soup - an onion does not make up a meal!<br /><br />From an Organisational Development perspective I try and think about basic motivation within roles that require cognitive function to be considerate of the three time-tested psychological concepts:<br /><br /> - Instinctual drives<br /> - Stimulus response<br />-  Shared intentions<br /><br />It might help for clarity to explain my view and own perspective:<br /><br />Instinctual drives - Nature or nurtured behavioral drivers are hard wired, hard to change, also influenced by culture or community - like a bird learning to fly or a cat wanting to hunt or our need to seek to satisfy Maslow's hierarchy of needs.<br /><br />Stimulus response - cause an effect, I avoid fire I don't get burnt, I eat I don't feel hungry, I work hard to achieve a goal - I get a rewarded, I don't achieve a goal - I don't get rewarded, I practice something - I get better at it, I work on this project (altruistic or otherwise) - I feel good helping.<br /><br />Shared intentions is about \\"fit and belonging to something to achieve something\\" - I want to be part of something great, I want to be on a winning team, I want to work with smart people to achieve something I'd value.<br /><br />I completely believe in the value of his \\"autonomy, mastery and purpose.\\" But when it comes to money pink missed the point, its not about the cash, it about how the cash (as an ingredient in the soup) helps to achieve our instinctual drives, eg; the need to fulfill our most basic fundamental needs; (Maslow's safety) provide for the family, health, shelter, food, even fun etc. Then stimulus response applies - I know if I achieve a goal I will be rewarded which will help me achieve some of my instinctual drives. If an employee's instinctual drives are not being fulfilled and they believe money will in part help resolve this - they become disengaged and leave for higher remuneration. Obviously there are other causal factors of instinctual drives not being met including how their manager treats them; whether they receive acknowledgement, respect, have the ability to create, solve problems etc. This is not debated by Pink.<br /><br />He mentions in his book countless studies to prove his theory - but the \\"countless\\" are not mentioned and there are many, countless studies demonstrating the alignment that cause and affect of having a range of rewards including amongst many; money aligned to the achievement of a goal to satisfy basic human needs and how this delivers value to an organisation. Its also important to note that most people don't grow up with the same experience as Pink, he had a privileged upbringing, he was a Truman scholar, Juris Dr from Yale, editor of the Yale Law review and was Al Gore's chief speech writer... He is one of the 1%. Money, the need for it and how it fulfills basic, fundamental needs is not something he truly understands.<br /><br />I believe Pink set out to write a best seller, he has brilliantly made a calculated, political attempt to tap into the anti-profit, anti-greed, wall street = bad sentiment that was prolific whilst writing Drive in 2009. I tell others to remember - he was Al Gore's chief speech writer and he was exceptional at it! But he has basically told us to eat the onion, not the soup.<br /><br />I'm giving this a bad review - not beacuse its not an interesting read but because just recently I had to try and convince an organisation that adopting his glossy \\"no cash incentive\\" approach would be disastrous for them and their environment. Applying \\"POP Leadership\\" like this is simply going to send a lot of businesses to the wall!<br /><br />Happy to debate further<br /><br />- John Me just recently I had to try and convince an organisation that adopting his glossy \\"no cash incentive\\" approach would be disastrous for them and their environment. Applying \\"POP Leadership\\" like this is simply going to send a lot of businesses to the wall!<br /><br />Happy to debate further<br /><br />- John M

0857520652|4.0|Former stage actress and BBC Radio writer Rachel Joyce has fashioned a novel of personal discovery that will leave you emotionally invested from the very first chapter. Drawing on spiritual antecedents such as the well-known [[ASIN:0743453255 Forrest Gump]] and the less-known but equally relevant [[ASIN:098430813X Amazing Adventures of a Nobody]], The Unlikely Pilgrimage of Harold Fry is a British walking adventure--\\"Life was very different when you walked through it.\\"--that offers descriptive prose with commensurately good dialogue. There's even a trail of [[ASIN:0877017883 Griffin & Sabine]]-like correspondence. The reveal is heartachingly shocking. Not until the final chapter does the scent go cold. I suspect the author was unsure how to tie off loose ends. In the real world, relationships are not always mended satisfactorily, and I would have preferred for art to imitate life in this case.<br /><br />>>Harold took the A roads, B roads, lanes and tracks. The compass quivered northward and he followed. He went by day or by night, as the mood took him; mile after mile after mile. If the blisters were bad, he bound them with duct tape. He slept when the need for it came, and then returned to his feet and walked again. He went under the stars, and the tender light of the moon, when it hung like an eyelash and the tree trunks shone like bones. He walked through wind and weather, and beneath sun-bleached skies. It seemed to Harold that he had been waiting all his life to walk.&lt;&lt;<br /><br />[The reviewer was provided with a complimentary copy of the book.]

1592332803|3.0|This book's strong points are also its weak points, depending on what your priorities are. As other reviewers have mentioned, the author generally makes simple substitutions from standard baking - usually margarine for butter and ener-g or tofu for eggs. The upside of this 'lowest common denominator' style of vegan baking is that it's very easy and inexpensive to find substitutes, and it won't alter your routine too much if you're used to standard baking. Still, it reminds me of a vegan cookbook that gives 'meat and potatoes' recipes with just tofu or seitan instead of the meat, in that it wears out its usefulness and gets boring pretty quickly. It helped me when I began baking vegan goodies, although I never really baked with eggs and dairy (I didn't know how to bake, period, before I bought this book to start learning). The sections that explain how to make substitutions and how leavening and binding agents work were helpful, but I imagine that other books or websites give similar information.<br /><br />I consider this book a decent buy if you want to make sweets as close to their egg- and dairy-containing counterparts and possible, with minimum substitution; this is especially handy if you're baking to share with people who might be leery of chocolate cake made with zucchini or something such. It also doesn't ask you to use chickpea and spelt flour and other expensive, hard-to-find ingredients that are so annoyingly common in vegan recipes (there is an over-reliance on 'pure maple syrup', however; does she not know how prohibitively expensive that stuff is? I use agave instead). If you really want to save your money, I recommend using vegan recipe websites and blogs rather than buying this book. Great ones to try include the Post-Punk Kitchen, Vegan Dad, Fat-Free Vegan Kitchen, and No-Meat Athlete; they don't have the same baking substitution tutorials as a cookbook (at least, not that I know of), but they generally have great basic recipes as well as more varied, innovative ones...plus, it's free and the commenters often give nifty tips and tricks. For the record, this author publishes recipes on her website too, but you have to pay for a subscription to access them. I don't necessarily fault her for it (she has to pay the bills too), but I'd rather try out the recipes that authors offer for free...then buy their cookbooks if they're good. Thank you, Isa Chandra Moskowitz!

0374275637|5.0|Winner of the Nobel Prize in Economics, Daniel Kahneman tells us that we use intuition all the time--and often well--to make daily decisions. But sometimes, even at important times, we use intuition and make very bad and harmful decisions. Getting up in age these days, Kahneman appears to have written this opus magnum as a great compilation of his research and that of his much admired academic partner, Amos Teversky (now deceased).  A leader in psychology and specifically decision making and behavioral economics, Kahneman explores two systems of how we think. System 1 is the quick-to-judge, emotional, and intuitive thinking system we all seem to possess and use often. System 2 is the slower, more deliberate and logical kind of thinking we also all possess. Kahneman profiles the amazingly fast and omnipresent power and influence of intuitive thinking...both its profound contribution to daily life and its hidden traps when making big decisions too quickly--fueled by the biases of System 1 thinking. He shows how anchoring, loss aversion, and various cognitive biases can powerfully affect our decision making at home, at work and in the marketplace. At the same time, he introduces us to the slower, more logical, and more deliberate partner, System 2, and how it can be used to moderate, even mitigate, the flaws in the fast-thinking System 1. In essence, he helps us understand when and where to use each of these systems to maximize our outcomes. His bottom line advice: When we make important decisions, we need to slow down.<br /><br />Reviewed in depth over the next two weeks on Survival Leadership Blog.

0345525558|5.0|The Language of Flowers is a powerful story about forgiveness, guilt and finding a place called home. As well as the meanings flowers hold.<br /><br />The story is told through the main character, Victoria. It starts on her eighteenth birthday as she leaves her old life at a group home and entering a new life as an adult to fend for herself. But she has not relied on anyone but herself for years and seeks a life of solitude. After not finding work, she spends some time homeless finding solace with her love. Her love of flowers. Until she learns she cannot survive without food and shelter. She then proves her knowledge as well as talent with flowers in front of a florist and begins working for her. While working, she meets a man who begins sending her messages with flowers. Only the two of them seem to know the forgotten language of flowers that was popular in the Victorian era. While conversing with the man with flowers, she is reminded of someone from her past and how she learned the language in the first place. Only this is something that has haunted her for years and she has never come to terms with. She has been running from it for years but can run no longer.<br /><br />The story flashes from the present to the past throughout the book allowing the reader to you see the complete picture closer to the end. Victoria's life was a hard one and the past has affected the woman she is in the present. The audience gets to see her love of flowers as she reestablishes the language of flowers in the present era and more importantly what the flowers represent to her in her various stages of life.<br /><br />Vanessa Diffenbaugh did a brilliant job portraying people especially our flaws. This is a heartfelt story about dealing and facing the past. Victoria's view was shown so well you felt what she felt and hurt alongside her. I enjoyed seeing her character grow and imagining the woman she will one day be. It was also a look into how our lives/past shape our characters for better or worse and the importance of having someone by our sides that never give up on us. One of Victoria's main flaws is one we all have. Sometimes we don't truly see ourselves. The self image a lot of people have is much lower than it should be. It was important at one point in this novel for her character to realize how other people saw her and that they saw goodness.<br /><br />Even, though this is a different style book than I usually read, I loved it. I'm so glad I read it. I enjoyed reading what the flowers meant and the story seemed so real to me. Not far-fetched like some. This book actually made me cry it was so good. You know the author's done something right when the story starts the waterworks not because one of the characters died but because there is just so much emotion in the story. If you want a story that makes you feel, this is the book for you.<br /><br />Recommend to anyone who wants a great story, a heartfelt plot or an interest in flowers.<br /><br />Favorite Quotes:<br />\\"It's kind of ironic, don't you think?\\" he asked.<br />\\"Here you are, obsessed with romantic language-a language invented for expression between lovers-and you use it to spread animosity.\\"<br /><br />\\"Common thistle is everywhere,\\" she said. \\"Which is perhaps why human beings are so relentlessly unkind to one another.\\"<br /><br />*Although I won a free copy of this book through a Goodreads first reads giveaway, my review is not altered. 0385344228|4.0|This novel kept me entertained and engaged, and its characters remained on my mind during periods of interruption.  While other reviewers seem to have encountered a similar plot before, I had not, and therefore found the subject matter and storyline relatively fresh (particularly the unusual ending).  The author is good with language, and captures a depth of feeling that is refreshing and unusual in courtroom drama/thrillers; in this sense, he achieves something comparable to Scott Turow's better writings.<br /><br />Frankly, the novel is unnerving to read, because it validates the quiet uneasiness that increasingly pervades our national awareness - that we really don't know what is going on in the lives of our children.  It is with distressing stirrings of self-recognition that we witness the actions and inactions of the narrator and his wife in their roles as parents: ignoring and/or rationalizing the obvious in an effort to avoid household tension; consoling themselves with that old bromide, \\"that's just teenagers for you\\"; fooling themselves with the belief that simply loving a child is enough.  Although we too often pretend otherwise, this is not the country we grew up in, in which respect, kindness, and self-control were demanded in the classroom, and enforced beyond its walls by any competent adult neighbor, shopkeeper, or passer-by within earshot.  This book and Jodi Picoult's \\"Nineteen Minutes\\" are uncomfortable, but valuable, shots across the bow.<br /><br />That said, I cannot give the book full marks because of a certain awkwardness and improbability in its conception.  That this intelligent, active, apparently well-balanced couple could be so passive and clueless as parents was, for me, a tough sell.  I assume the author wanted them both to be likeable, and so spared us the sorts of characters in whom such incompetence might be consistent (e.g., greedy, career-obsessed people; country club social climbers addicted to the tennis court; alcoholics; drugged-out, overgrown hippies, etc.)<br /><br />Further, I do question the accuracy of the author's depiction of the operation of the criminal justice system in his state, and I take issue with his apparently allowing himself so much license in describing it.  I am not too proud to confess that I regard fiction as an enjoyable means of learning - about travel, science, technology, medicine, history.  I suspect that I am not alone in this tendency, and that there are readers who value courtroom novels as a means of adding to their store of information about the legal system.  I hope that Mr. Landay will consider, in future writings, that some readers may imbibe as factual his presentation of how things unfold during the course of criminal investigation and prosecution. To be fair to him, maybe that is how things are done in Massachusetts.  Seems doubtful, though.

Reese's character is a typical high school girl trying to assert her independence.  When her dad picks up on some disturbing signals The Boyfriend is sending out, he tries every trick in the book to discourage her from continuing to date the young man.  As every parent of teens knows, the best way to ensure that a relationship will become more committed is to DISAPPROVE of it, loudly and often. That's why, when your adolescent/young adult brings home a person who gives you the creeps, you play the role of supportive, loving parent totally enamored of the new interest, until your kid (hopefully) comes to his or her senses.<br /><br />Anyway. You just know bad things will happen, and they do.<br /><br />It is interesting that religion is only ever mentioned in movies if religion is central to the story (ie, \\"Sound of Music\\" and \\"Black Narcissus\\") or if it's a negative trait--another red flag that the religious person is a nutcase.
